<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:30:17)</span>: You're not implying that those numbers, that a previous witness quoted and are stated in your report, provides an absolute identification of Mr. Robinson or [Mr. Twiggs](/People/lance-twiggs)?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:30:29)</span>: No, I'm not.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:30:30)</span>: Okay. Thank you. Can you take that down? Are you also familiar with a concept called formal activity level?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:30:43)</span>: Yes, I am.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:30:43)</span>: Tell the court what that is.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:30:45)</span>: Formal activity level is determining an activity that causes DNA to be left behind on an object.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:30:55)</span>: And does your laboratory have a policy about whether you can express opinions about formal activity level?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:31:04)</span>: We do have a policy, and I cannot give an opinion as far as activity level goes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:31:10)</span>: And why is that?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:31:13)</span>: That is because DNA can be left behind on an object for numerous different reasons. So just because DNA is on an item doesn't necessarily mean that an individual touched it or utilized that item. So because of that, we do not opine on what activity may have occurred.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:31:33)</span>: And consistent with that policy, you cannot tell... If you interpret a piece of DNA as being present on an item, you can't tell when that DNA was deposited, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:31:44)</span>: I can't give an exact time, but there are some situations. If a piece of evidence has been buried for a long time or is in a river for a long time, it would be my expectation, based on my experience, that I probably won't get DNA off of an item that's been buried for 10 years, for example. However, I cannot give exact timing as to when DNA was deposited on an item.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:32:06)</span>: And I was talking more about a situation where you actually find DNA on an object, be it a gun or anything else. You can't say when that DNA was deposited, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:32:18)</span>: Sure, that's correct.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:32:21)</span>: And how long in general does the DNA persist on an item once you've made contact? How does DNA get deposited on an item in general?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:32:33)</span>: In general, DNA can be deposited a few different ways, by a body fluid, such as saliva or blood or skin cells. So touching an item or wearing an item, DNA could be deposited. Once DNA is on an item, if it's subjected to ideal conditions, it's out of heat and high UV, it's not subjected to any kind of cleaning or chemicals, it can persist on an item rather indefinitely. It can degrade slowly over time, but we've certainly worked items of evidence that are 40 and 50 years old, and we've been able to recover DNA.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:33:09)</span>: Now, could you explain to the court the concept of degradation of DNA samples, what that means and what it suggests with your testing?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:33:18)</span>: Sure. So degradation of DNA is breaking up the DNA. So if you think of DNA like a long rope, it's essentially taking that rope and cutting it up into pieces. Seeing degradation in a sample could lend me to believe that that sample may have been subjected to cleaning or high heat an extended period of time. So really, I can't tell why an item is degraded, but looking at my data, I can tell if that sample is potentially degraded or broken down.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:33:52)</span>: And two of the items that you examined were referred to as Items 7 and 8, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:33:59)</span>: That is correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:34:00)</span>: One was a towel and one was a screwdriver.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:34:03)</span>: That is correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:34:04)</span>: Were both those samples degraded to a certain extent?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:34:11)</span>: May I refer to my data? So Item 8 was the screwdriver from the perch. I would say that there was some degradation as I look at the DNA peaks or the DNA data. In an ideal world, if there's no degradation, I would expect all the peaks to be at the same height. Here, I see a ski slope-type effect, meaning that the data on the left is a little bit taller than the data on the right. As far as Item 7, Item 7 was the towel. There was slight amount of degradation, but not as much as Item 8.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:35:17)</span>: Okay. And what is the effect of degradation on production of the... First of all, what's an allele?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:35:27)</span>: An allele is an individual's DNA type. So we inherit half of our DNA from our mom and half of our DNA from our dad. We're looking at something called short tandem repeats, or STRs. These are small segments of DNA that repeat themselves over and over again. We all have those same sections of DNA, but what might differ amongst us is how many times it repeats. And that's actually what we inherit from our parents. So for example, at one particular location, my mom may give me 14 repeats, and my dad may give me 15. So when I'm looking at the DNA types, I would say that I have two alleles at that location, a 14 and a 15. As far as degradation goes, it could cause some of those alleles to do something called dropout, meaning when I'm looking at the DNA chart or the DNA data, I do not see information. I do not obtain numbers at that particular section of DNA.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:36:22)</span>: And is it true that in the testing that you were doing in this case, STR testing, what you were looking at is the length of the what I'll call... Have you ever heard the boxcar analogy?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:36:36)</span>: I have, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:36:36)</span>: Tell the court what that is.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:36:38)</span>: So the boxcar analogy is how many times that section of DNA repeats. So that section is essentially letters so, for example, ACTG. So you may have ACTG repeated 10 times or 12 times. Each of those sections of ACTG would be considered one boxcar.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:37:00)</span>: And so when you're looking for alleles, you're essentially counting the boxcars. Could be seven boxcars and that person would be a seven?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:37:10)</span>: That is correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:37:11)</span>: And from the mom and from the dad, he may have inherited 10 boxcars?

And this is looking at 21 different locations on a human's DNA, in addition to three sex determining locations. That lets me know if an individual is biologically a male or a female.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:46:33)</span>: Yes, I'm familiar with that one as well.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:46:35)</span>: And Forensic DNA Typing Interpretation?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:46:39)</span>: Everybody in your field relies on these publications and use them as teaching tools?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:46:44)</span>: Yes, I would agree with that.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:46:46)</span>: And then in addition to those books, there have been studies that have been done on particular topics, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:46:54)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:46:54)</span>: This case, we're dealing with mixtures, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:46:58)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:46:59)</span>: And mixtures present certain challenges that are not present when the samples are not mixed, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:47:06)</span>: Not necessarily. First, to define a mixture, that simply means DNA from more than one individual in a sample. So, if I were to take a sip from a coffee cup and my husband were to also take a sip, if I were to swab that coffee cup, I would get a mixture, his DNA and my DNA together. As far as it being difficult, you can have some mixtures where there's one contributor that donates a lot of DNA and the second contributor donates a little bit. For DNA interpretation, that is not challenging for me. Where it can become challenging is if you have four or five individuals within that sample all donating roughly around the same amount, it may be challenging for me to determine if a person is included or not.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:47:51)</span>: And would you also agree that that challenge exists in situations where you have what are called low quantities of DNA?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:48:02)</span>: Again, that could be possible. I could have low quantities of multiple individuals, which is simply just saying that I can't determine which piece of DNA goes with which individual, so that's possible.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:48:15)</span>: What is drop-in?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:48:17)</span>: Drop-in is where you have environmental DNA that's not inherent to that sample that's detected during our DNA process. DNA testing is very sensitive, so it could be that we have a peak that's detected, like I said, from the environment.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:48:36)</span>: And what is dropout?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:48:37)</span>: Dropout is when you have an allele that is not detected or a piece of DNA information that is not detected. In the DNA chart, there is no number associated with that particular location.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:48:51)</span>: Degradation can cause dropout?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:48:53)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:48:54)</span>: So, if you have a person at a particular location that is a type 14, 15 and because of degradation that 15 drops out, what you're seeing on your chart is just the 14, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:49:09)</span>: That is possible, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:49:11)</span>: And that presents a certain challenge, does it not? Because if the 15 is not there, you could misinterpret that as a type 14, 14, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:49:19)</span>: Not necessarily. We have thresholds. We have something called the stochastic threshold. If that 14 is above that stochastic threshold, I'm confident based on our validation, our testing and my training that the DNA type there is only a 14. If that 14 falls below that stochastic threshold, that could be indication that there is another peak. So when I'm interpreting, when I'm comparing if that 14 is below threshold and someone is a 14, 15, I will not automatically exclude them.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:49:55)</span>: What is stochastic effects?

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:49:59)</span>: I'm going to object at this point. I think we've gone beyond the scope of preliminary hearing. I think this is getting into 702 material that's not necessary for the probable cause determination.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:09)</span>: All right. Mr. Burt?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:10)</span>: I think it goes directly to the reliability of the testing results that the state introduced. I think the court has to understand limitations and potential sources of error in order to assess whether these results are reliable.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:28)</span>: And in regards to this line of questioning, how far are you going to go on this line of questioning?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:33)</span>: I have this one question and then I'm moving on.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:36)</span>: All right. Mr. McBride.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:39)</span>: I'll hold him to that.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:41)</span>: All right. Mr. Burt, so the objection is overruled. You may proceed with that question.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:48)</span>: You can answer.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:49)</span>: Could you repeat your question, please?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:50)</span>: Tell the court about stochastic effects.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:50:54)</span>: Stochastic effects, when you have low level samples, during the DNA copying process, based on how the DNA is copied, you could have alleles that are copied better than other alleles.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:51:13)</span>: On another topic, I mentioned studies before. One of the studies that you're familiar with is called the PCAST report. Are you familiar with that report?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:51:27)</span>: Yes, I am.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:51:28)</span>: Tell the court what that is.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:51:28)</span>: PCAST stands for the President's Council of Advisors on Science and Technology. And this was a committee that was found, I believe, under the Obama administration that gave guidance on different areas of forensics.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:51:45)</span>: Including how to deal with mixed samples and how to deal with samples that have low quantity of DNA.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:51:53)</span>: I don't know that I would agree that it's how to deal with them. They gave some of their concerns on low level samples and mixtures.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:52:06)</span>: And they also did a follow-up report to assess whether using the kind of software that you used in this case had been established as reliable, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:52:15)</span>: That is correct.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:52:17)</span>: What was the software that you used in this case to reach your statistical results?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:52:24)</span>: The software is called STRmix, S-T-R-M-I-X.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:52:30)</span>: The PCAST report specifically addresses whether reliability has been established with respect to STRmix, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:52:40)</span>: I don't believe reliability came into question. I think PCAST thought that there needed to be additional testing as far as mixtures and low level samples go.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:52:54)</span>: Mixtures and low level samples that had certain characteristics, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:52:58)</span>: That is correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:52:59)</span>: Including mixtures where what's called the minor contributor was in the sample below 20%?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:53:09)</span>: That is correct. The minor contributor would be an individual that donated less DNA to that sample. PCAST called into question mixtures of three or more individuals with that minor or that low level contributor making up 20% or less.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:53:27)</span>: Seven and eight samples in this case were both mixtures, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:53:30)</span>: Yes, that is correct.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:53:31)</span>: And did you determine what the proportion of mixture was between what I'll call the major contributor and the minor contributor?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:53:41)</span>: I did not calculate that personally. Part of the STRmix software, it does a couple different things. One, it takes a mixture and it uses math and biological modeling to try to break that mixture into its individual contributors. And then it also calculates a likelihood ratio, which is the statistic that I report. During that process, it does give an estimate of the breakdown of those potential contributors.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:54:09)</span>: First of all, for the seven sample, was the minor in there at less than 20% according to the STRmix?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:54:24)</span>: Yes, it was. For one of the runs when I compared to Mr. Robinson conditioning on Mr. Twiggs, it was a 5% to 95%.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:54:35)</span>: And how about for the other sample?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:54:50)</span>: For item eight, one of the contributors was 11% and the other was approximately 89%.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:54:56)</span>: So...

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:54:57)</span>: Correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:54:59)</span>: And the concern expressed in this report, which was written by experts in your field, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:55:06)</span>: No, that is not correct. The PCAST report was written by individuals that were not practitioners or any type of laboratory personnel.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:55:16)</span>: But they list the experts who provided input into that report, do they not?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:55:20)</span>: Yes, they did.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:55:21)</span>: One of them was Bruce Budowle, correct?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:55:25)</span>: Who's Bruce Budowle?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:55:28)</span>: He is a DNA expert within the field.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:55:31)</span>: Well, he was the head of your unit for a number of years before you came on board, wasn't he?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:55:36)</span>: He was, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:55:38)</span>: And he's written extensively on forensic DNA testing, has he not?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:55:41)</span>: He has, yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:56:03)</span>: Objection, Your Honor. I'm going to renew my objection. I think this is beyond the scope of determining probable cause. The standards that apply here are that the court weighs the evidence in the light most favorable to the state and avoids credibility and balancing the evidence at this stage. I think we're far into 702 territory at this point.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:56:23)</span>: Mr. Burt.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:56:25)</span>: I'm not sure I understand the objection into 702 territory. 702 deals with reliability of evidence. That's what the court is here to determine. So again, it's directly relevant to reliability. This report criticizes for the specific samples she's using here, the reliability of this testing. And her response was, "Oh, but they weren't experts." But the report indicates that there were experts who were consulted on this report, so I think it is relevant and I think it goes to the issue of reliability. And it's the only question I have on this area as well.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:57:05)</span>: Anything further, Mr. McBride, or do you wish to respond to that?

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:57:10)</span>: Just that 702 deals with admissibility. It does discuss reliability, but it's directly related to admissibility of the testimony at trial under the Utah Constitution. And under the robust case law in this area, the court does not weigh the evidence, draws the inferences in the light most favorable to the state. I think we're beyond that point now getting into the admissibility questions.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:57:42)</span>: All right. Anything further, Mr. Burt?

Well, as this is the last question, I'm going to overrule the objection, but as a reminder to all parties that this is a probable cause standard and as it relates to... Well, I will just leave it at that.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:58:05)</span>: Are you familiar with that analyst?

Isn't that what they concluded?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:58:45)</span>: That sounds accurate, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:58:47)</span>: Do you know why the level 20% raises reliability issues?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:59:10)</span>: That's the next thing I was going to ask you about, which is what is NoC?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:59:16)</span>: NoC stands for Number of Contributors.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:59:20)</span>: And when you use this program, this genotyping program, STRmix, who decides what the number of contributors is?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:59:30)</span>: That's my determination based on my experience and training looking at the data. That's something I input into STRmix.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:59:41)</span>: That's a complex determination, is it not?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:59:45)</span>: Not necessarily.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:59:48)</span>: But it can be in some cases, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(05:59:50)</span>: It could be. Again, if there's mixtures of a lot of individuals and a lot of the peak heights, the size of the spikes are relatively the same, it could cause some trouble for me as an examiner.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:00:10)</span>: One of the groups that studies your field is the National Institute of Standards and Technology. Correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:00:19)</span>: That is correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:00:28)</span>: They published a report in 2024 called Forensic DNA Interpretation and Human Factors: Improving Practice Through a Systems Approach. <span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:01:38)</span>: Objection. Same objection, Your Honor. This is beyond the scope of a preliminary hearing. Again, we're talking about expert reports. We're talking about scientific studies. These materials are all fine to consider at a 702 admissibility hearing. <span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:02:08)</span>: Thank you, Mr. McBride. Mr. Burt.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:02:11)</span>: Your Honor, I think the report that the state put into evidence as clarified by this witness indicates that she reached the conclusion that Mr. Robinson and Mr. Twiggs were, quote, "possible contributors" to these two samples. One thing the court has to weigh is what is the weight of that opinion given the science or lack thereof?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:02:38)</span>: One of the important issues here is how she derived these statistical numbers. As an offer of proof, I'll indicate to the court that the numbers derived from this software program... And that that software program is dependent in large part on how many contributors the analyst puts into the input of the software. And the numbers can change if the number of contributors is changed. In this case, as I'll show through the examination, this examiner originally thought there were three contributors. And I will show to the court why she changed her analysis and will indicate through this publication that there has been criticism of the way in which she determined the number of contributors.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:03:37)</span>: So, I think it's important for the court to assess whether you've got accurate numbers in front of you. The state put in these astronomical numbers and are asking the court to give weight to them. And I'm asking the court to consider how those numbers were arrived at and whether there are reliability issues with regard to those numbers.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:04:00)</span>: The concern that I have is... I turn to State v. Ramirez 2012 UT 59 at paragraph 10. It states, "Furthermore, the magistrate's role does not encompass an assessment of whether such inference is more plausible than an alternative that cuts in favor of the defense. That is a matter-of-fact finding which is left for the jury at trial." So, when I'm staying in my role as a magistrate, I hear what you're saying, but my concern is based off case law that dictates what I as a magistrate should be doing. If I want to stay in that lane, my fear is as we go down further down this path, which again is not precluding if this were to proceed, if there were a bind over, if there was a trial, that certainly that could be determined. But at this point, at this stage, how do you weigh this issue as I'm weighing State v. Ramirez in deciding whether we're going past the probable cause stage?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:05:02)</span>: Sure, and I think the answer to that is we have to take into account the ruling that you made in this case, which is the law of this case, which is when we objected to the hearsay aspect of these DNA reports and other reports, you ruled that you are the ultimate determiner of the reliability of the hearsay evidence being admitted. You will recall that our argument was the statute was unconstitutional because it was dictating that you find certain evidence to be reliable without considering whether it was reliable. Your ruling, as I understood it, was no, you retain the ultimate determination of whether the evidence is reliable. And that's not a credibility issue. It's, is this reliable hearsay that the state is offering?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:05:58)</span>: Issues that go to whether these numbers that the state is putting in front of you are reliable, I think go directly to the substance of your ruling, which is that your role here is to determine reliability of evidence. I'm familiar with a case that the court cites, but I think that has more to do with weighing evidence and making credibility assessments, whereas I'm focused on what the court ruled, which is we have to look at reliability here. And that's my job as a magistrate.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:06:32)</span>: Mr. McBride, any final thoughts?

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:06:35)</span>: Yes. Under Rule 1102(b)(4) the Exhibit 31 is considered reliable hearsay. In addition, credibility determinations are not proper for the court to make here for the whole reason that studies supporting this science, studies opposing this science, testimony from this expert or that expert about what is reliable, what is not, what's more reliable, what's less reliable, factors that affect all those determinations, those are all very, very complex decisions. There will be probably multiple-day hearings dealing with that. And a probable cause determination is just not the place to conduct that kind of thorough, in-depth, and searching analysis, rather the case law, the constitution of Utah that says the only purpose of a preliminary hearing is determining probable cause.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:07:34)</span>: The case law, the rules that all say the court does not weigh the evidence at this stage, but leaves that for a finder of fact, the jury in the end, that all supports cutting this line of questioning off at this point rather than going on into greater depth, Your Honor.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:07:55)</span>: All right. Based off what's been presented before me, I'm going to sustain in part and deny in part the objection made. I'm going to allow this question to be asked. So, that is denying, but sustained in the sense that this would be the last question. And if Mr. Burt chooses to, may go on to a different subject or a different line of questioning.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:08:22)</span>: Sure. Did that NIST report conclude that it remains unclear if visual inspection renders consistent number of contributor estimations across analysts over time?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:08:38)</span>: Can you repeat that?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:08:39)</span>: Yeah. Did the NIST report that you referenced conclude that it remains unclear if visual inspection renders consistent number of contributor estimations across analysts over time?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:08:58)</span>: I think that goes to the DNA evidence. Again, we never truly know the number of contributors in a forensic sample. Like I mentioned before, you could have DNA mixtures that are made up of two, three, four individuals. I have training, I have experience, I followed standard operating procedures, so I'm making the best determination based on the DNA evidence that I have as far as number of contributors goes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:09:28)</span>: Now if I could, I'd like to return to your case notes. And could you display for the witness page 365 of tab number four? And while he's doing that, by the way, it's true that if you change the number of contributors, you're going to get different numbers from the software program?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:09:51)</span>: It depends. So actually, each time you run the software, you will get a different number based on the math that the software system does. But there are studies and validations that show we don't expect that math to be greatly different, usually by a factor of 10. Changing the number of contributors, it depends. If you have a major contributor, someone who's made up most of that DNA profile, if I originally interpret something as three, calculated statistic, if that major contributor is the individual that I'm comparing to, if I change the number of contributors to two, based on the studies and based on my own experience, it doesn't change the likelihood ratio of that major contributor by much.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:10:37)</span>: But it does change it, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:10:38)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:10:39)</span>: And you just said, I think, that even when you put in the same parameters, you don't get the same answer if you run it through STRmix more than once, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:10:49)</span>: That is correct. Based on how STRmix utilizes math and distributions, you will never get the same answer twice, but it will be no more than a magnitude of 10.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:11:03)</span>: That test is not reproducible in the sense that you can run a sample or your assumptions in there 10 times, you could get 10 different answers?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:11:15)</span>: That is true. The first time I run STRmix, I'm not going to get a likelihood ratio of 10 and the second time get 10 quadrillion. I might get 10 quadrillion the first time, two quadrillion the second time. But even if I run it 10 times, I'm still going to get around the same likelihood ratio value.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:11:34)</span>: Could you look at tab number four, your case notes at page 365? Before I issue a report, before I write a report, I review all of the case notes. So that will be any communication log entries, any documentation from the laboratory, the biologists that perform the DNA testing. I will review all of that, write a report and then give that to a technical reviewer. They will draw their own conclusions to determine if they agree with me. If they do, they will sign off. And then my report and my case file will go for an administrative review to make sure that the laboratory number is correct on all the pages, that if there's any cross-outs, there's dates and initials and so forth. Those two reviews have to be done before I can actually make it a final report and case file that gets submitted and sent to the field.

For this situation, I had DNA evidence coming to me throughout the day, throughout the night. So I had reviewers at different steps, which is not usual for a case. Usually I have one reviewer. <span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:23:42)</span>: All right. Mr. Burt?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:23:44)</span>: Your Honor, I think this exhibit is particularly important because the court does not yet have in front of it what an electropherogram is and how it's interpreted. And this particular page will illustrate to the court how the analyst interpreted the peaks on this electropherogram to reach the conclusions that she reached that there were three and not two contributors. And I think it will assist the court in determining exactly how this comparison process works and what the output looks like so the court can see exactly how the interpretations are being made.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:24:23)</span>: And is this the... So I'm viewing State's Exhibit 31 as a summary and this more of showing the work of how the summary was reached. Am I misunderstanding how this fits in?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:24:39)</span>: This fits into how the analyst originally was of the opinion that there are three people here and not two. And she will go on to explain how she changed the interpretation from three to two. And the point of this is to illustrate to the court that this is a subjective process to a certain extent and that people can read these things in different ways. And so I think it will help the court understand exactly what's being compared here. You've got peaks on a line, she will say... And just let the court know in response to Mr. McBride's statement that he was unaware that this is going to be an issue. He and I met with this analyst before the preliminary hearing. The analyst pointed this page out to us as being illustrative of why she originally concluded that there were three contributors. So this is not something that's being sprung on the state. He was a part of that conversation. And this particular page was identified by the analyst, not by me. So it is important to understand why we went from three to two and what impact that has on the numbers that the state is offering.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:26:14)</span>: All right. Under Utah Rule of Evidence 1102B4, it says, "Scientific Laboratory of Forensic Reports and Records," I find that this is admissible and is admitted into evidence as Bakker 4, page 128. And is there a request to publish this document?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:26:45)</span>: Yes. Correct.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:26:50)</span>: All right. It may be published. Counsel, just as a reminder, as this is a probable cause, I understand and I appreciate you giving me a pointer of where it's going, but just as a reminder as probable cause it is admitted and you may discuss it, but I definitely want to keep this within the track of probable cause.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:27:13)</span>: Understood. Thank you.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:27:15)</span>: Thank you, Mr. Burt.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:27:19)</span>: Okay. Is it true what I just told the court that you pointed this particular page out as illustrating why you originally thought we had three contributors?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:27:30)</span>: Yes, that is true.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:27:30)</span>: So explain to the court, is this an electropherogram?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:27:34)</span>: Yes, this is an electropherogram. Each of those rectangles at the top, for example, D2S441, that is that small segment of DNA that I referred to earlier. And each of those peaks has a corresponding number that is the allele. So that corresponds to the number of repeats that an individual inherited.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:27:57)</span>: And this is what you ultimately ... You get this electropherogram, not through running it through STRmix. There's a different device that you use to produce these electropherograms, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:28:12)</span>: Correct. Yes. During the DNA process, this is the last step of that process. So what it's doing is it's ... DNA is found in cells of our body. We're taking those cells, we're breaking them open with chemicals to release the DNA from those cells. We're making photocopies of those pieces of DNA so that we can essentially see them. And then the last step is to separate those pieces by size. And the ultimate output is this electropherogram.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:28:40)</span>: Okay. And is it true that you set the parameters for what the machine will pop up in terms of numbering the alleles?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:28:55)</span>: Personally, I do not set that up. That was done during our validation of the chemicals and the software and the instrument that we utilize. But there are parameters. So the output of the DNA lets me know, for example, the different types of alleles. But that's not a parameter I set.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:29:15)</span>: I want to make sure you answered that. You set or your lab sets what the machine does in terms of labeling particular alleles, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:29:27)</span>: Yes. That's part of the whole process and the validation. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:29:31)</span>: So you could tell the machine, "Give me all the peaks at whatever level and label them as whatever the alleles are"?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:29:41)</span>: I see. So yes. So during our validation, we determine our thresholds, our analytical threshold, and that stochastic threshold that I mentioned before. The analytical threshold is a threshold that was determined during validation that we input into the software that I utilize that says, for example, our analytical threshold is 150. Any peak that is 150 or greater, the software system will give me that allele call or that number for that peak. Any peak that's below the 150, the software system will not give me an allele call for it.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:18)</span>: Even though it might be a true allele?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:21)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:22)</span>: So you could say... And you referred to a measurement, you refer to that as RFUs, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:29)</span>: Yes, that is the unit.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:31)</span>: What does that mean?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:33)</span>: It stands for relative fluorescent unit.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:36)</span>: So in your lab, you tell a machine, "Only label peaks that come above 150 RFU"?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:44)</span>: The software that utilizes the information from the machine, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:48)</span>: However, it's true, is it not, that in determining the number of contributors, you look below that 150 RFU level?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:58)</span>: And that is true, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:30:59)</span>: All right. Now on this, this is only showing one, two, three, four different locations, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:31:05)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:31:06)</span>: And you see a number of peaks at each one of these locations. Some of them are labeled, some of them are not.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:31:14)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:31:15)</span>: So tell us how you originally looked at this and said there's not two contributors, there's three.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:31:21)</span>: Sure. So first what I do is I count how many labels that I see, so how many of those little squares under each of those locations. So for example, at D2S441, there are three squares. Knowing that one piece of information comes from mom and one comes from dad, if it's a single source profile from one individual, I expect to see no more than two squares. Because I see three, that gives me an indication that there's at least two individuals. Same thing for the next, D19S433, and as well as FGA. There were three peaks that were called. So I knew this had to be at least two individuals. Then what I do is I take a look at the peaks that are not called and determine could there potentially be a third person included? Because I'm able to use information below the threshold and I see peaks, that gives me an indication that I can increase my number of contributors to three, which I did in this case.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:32:56)</span>: Okay. And when did you reach that conclusion, time-wise?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:33:03)</span>: If I recall, it was somewhere around September 12th.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:33:07)</span>: And when did you change your conclusion?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:33:30)</span>: I changed my conclusion on September 13th of 2025.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:33:49)</span>: And explain to the court why you changed your conclusion.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:33:52)</span>: I was submitted an elimination sample from Mr. [Twiggs](/People/lance-twiggs). And an elimination sample is a sample in which it's from a known individual, and then there's an expectation that the individual's DNA might be present. In receiving that sample and talking to the investigators, it was decided that Mr. Twiggs should be used as an elimination. When I compared him to this sample, all of the DNA, unlike that major contributor, matched to Mr. Twiggs. So because of that, I determined that there's actually only two individuals, because those small peaks, there was nothing left for a third individual to be compared to.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:34:32)</span>: You use a phrase elimination sample. That's typically used, is it not, in a situation where you're testing a sample that has more than one contributor?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:34:48)</span>: Not necessarily. An elimination is just simply an individual that we're expecting their DNA to be there. So for example, if we have a stolen vehicle, I will request an elimination sample from the known driver of the vehicle so I can subtract out their DNA to see what I have left over. It doesn't necessarily have to be more than one individual. There certainly has been times where I only detect that known expected individual.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:35:15)</span>: And you said you had a conversation with the investigators as to whether Twiggs should be included as... Or you talked to them about whether you should get elimination samples?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:35:32)</span>: I talked to them with regards to if I should utilize Mr. Twiggs as an elimination versus comparing him and offering a statistic.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:35:41)</span>: Okay. But in your report you concluded, did you not, that one of the contributors to both samples was Twiggs?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:35:55)</span>: That is correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:35:57)</span>: Right. You didn't just assume that, you reached the determination that he was the source of both those samples, one of the sources, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:36:07)</span>: I don't want to use the word source. But knowing that there was an expectation that his DNA could be present when I compared him to that DNA sample, I did see his DNA types present. So using the word assumed, that's verbiage that we use when we calculate our statistical calculation. I'm letting the software know I know this individual is there. I'm assuming that they're there. Based on my comparisons I did see evidence that that person was there.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:36:39)</span>: But I guess what I'm confused about is we talked before about how your language should never imply an absolute identification, but in the body of your report, you say that one of the contributors is Twiggs. You don't say he's a possible contributor, like you do for Mr. Robinson, you say he is a contributor. Do you not?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:37:00)</span>: I do. And with elimination samples, that's slightly different. So for example, let's say I have a sexual assault case and I have a vaginal swab from a female. She has a consensual partner. I can request an elimination sample from that consensual partner. If I see that they're present, I can say they're present and say the DNA foreign to that victim and that consensual partner is one person, for example. So it's different than comparing a known individual if they're an elimination sample. I can say that I expect their DNA to be there. And based on my interpretation and reviewing that data, they are present.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:37:41)</span>: And based either on your conversations with the agents or other people, why did you think that Twiggs was someone whose DNA you would expect in those two samples?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:37:54)</span>: So from what I recall in speaking with the investigators, Mr. Twiggs was a roommate of Mr. Robinson. And it was thought that those items came from potentially Mr. Robinson's home. And I don't recall exactly if it was a situation where a particular towel was known to have been in that house. I don't remember that exactly. But in talking to the investigators, based on their investigation, it was important to assume potentially Mr. Twiggs was present.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:38:27)</span>: All right. Did any investigator ever tell you that the towel did not come from Mr. Robinson's home but was found in a bush out at the university?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:38:37)</span>: I was aware of that, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:38:40)</span>: So you knew that that towel was not found in a place where you would inspect Mr. Twiggs' DNA to be present?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:38:48)</span>: That is correct. It was found outside of a home. But again, based on speaking with the investigators, they had information to suspect that that towel came from Mr. Robinson's home and I should utilize Mr. Twiggs as an elimination.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:39:04)</span>: All right. And again, you didn't assume that, you actually concluded it based on the match, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:39:11)</span>: Based on reviewing Mr. Twiggs' DNA profile to what I developed from these items, I used him as an elimination sample.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:39:18)</span>: Now you did various STRmix, what I'll call runs, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:39:27)</span>: That is correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:39:28)</span>: And the way this works is you input the number of contributors. You determine that. And then they also have something called HD and HP, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:39:43)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:39:44)</span>: Tell the court what that is.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:39:45)</span>: So those are different propositions. So in calculating the likelihood ratio, likelihood ratio compares the probabilities of seeing a DNA profile given one of two different scenarios or explanations or hypotheses. One is that a particular person of interest is a contributor and the other is that an unknown unrelated individual is a contributor. So depending on the question being asked is how I set up STRmix, number of contributors. For example, if I'm utilizing someone as a known elimination sample, I will apply their DNA profile both to the HP and HD.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:40:23)</span>: And the theory of this HP is the prosecution hypothesis, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:40:28)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:40:29)</span>: And HD is the defense hypothesis?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:40:32)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:40:35)</span>: And these are things you plug in based on your understanding of what the prosecution and the defense are contending?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:40:42)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:40:42)</span>: So how do you plug in the HD in a case where you don't yet have a defense postulating what their theory is?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:40:50)</span>: So HD or the defense hypothesis is simply the DNA profile, the probability of the DNA profile, given that it's another unknown unrelated person to the prosecution's potential person of interest.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:41:07)</span>: Mr. Burt, I don't want to interrupt your line of questions, but we've been going for just shy of two hours. Would right now be a good time to take a 15—

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:41:14)</span>: It'd be a great time, Your Honor. Thank you.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(06:41:16)</span>: All right, let's take a 15 minute break. We'll come back at 3:50. The court is in a brief recess.

*(Afternoon recess.)*

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:03:46)</span>: We are still with Mr. Burt and Ms. Bakker on direct examination. Thank you. Mr. Burt, your witness.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:03:55)</span>: Ms. Bakker, would you take a look at in your notes page 24 of 379? <span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:04:56)</span>: So at that time, I believe I ran statistics with just Twiggs comparing that individual to the sample. <span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:05:43)</span>: Okay. And could you go to Tab 38, page 39? And this is a... page 39. Is this the DNA report? I'm sorry, the STRmix report that was produced as a result of your inquiry that involved a comparison of the sample to Mr. Twiggs alone?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:06:32)</span>: Yes, it is.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:06:33)</span>: And is that a six-page report?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:06:40)</span>: And is it a true and accurate copy of the STRmix report for that particular sample?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:11:14)</span>: Okay. And then you input that data and what you get out from that is a likelihood ratio, right?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:11:22)</span>: So let's go to page two of that report. And is this a summary of the results from that test on the 15th in terms of a likelihood ratio?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:11:34)</span>: Yes, it is.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:11:35)</span>: What is a likelihood ratio? What does that mean?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:11:38)</span>: A likelihood ratio is a statistic that calculates the probability of seeing a DNA profile given one of two different hypotheses. One is that a particular person of interest is a contributor and the other is that an unknown unrelated individual is a contributor. The probability with the higher number gets the support and the magnitude of the support is determined by the ratio of those two numbers.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:12:04)</span>: That's pretty technical stuff, but the bottom line is that the software computes the numbers and then you look at those numbers and determine whether it has any significance, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:12:19)</span>: I don't know that I would say if it has significance. I report the value that's calculated.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:12:24)</span>: Right. And why is one column... These columns relate to different what are called population databases, right?

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:13:03)</span>: Your Honor, at this point, I'm going to object on, again, grounds of relevance to probable cause hearing. State v. Drozbeck, State v. Merrill, State v. Ramirez that the court has cited all hold that the court should bind the defendant over for trial unless the evidence is wholly lacking and incapable of reasonable inference to prove some issue as supports the prosecution's claim. I don't know that any of this analysis goes to whether or not the expert's testimony and her report is wholly lacking and incapable of reasonable inference to prove her result in this case. We're far beyond this. We're hours into this examination. This feels like a 702 hearing to me. I think we're far beyond a probable cause hearing, Your Honor. I would ask the court to sustain the objection, not allow questioning on this or any other matter that does not deal with the issue of probable cause.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:14:07)</span>: Thank you, Mr. McBride. Mr. Burt?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:14:10)</span>: Your Honor, the state offered this report on a very technical, complex scientific area of inquiry. And they offered to the court a conclusion in that report that Mr. Robinson and Mr. Twiggs were contributors to two samples that were found at the crime scene. They obviously think that's relevant to the probable cause inquiry or they wouldn't have offered the report in the first place. So having done that, I think the defense is entitled to inquire whether those conclusions are reliable because that, again, is the court's ultimate determination here, reliability of this evidence. And the court can decide it's not reliable, therefore I'm not going to rely on that in determining probable cause. Or the court could say it is reliable and it bolsters up other evidence that the court is going to hear from. So the particular report in front of us goes directly to the issue of whether the expert correctly concluded that Twiggs was not a possible contributor, but was a contributor. And the state's theory here apparently is that both Mr. Robinson and Mr. Twiggs are somehow connected to this crime scene. So I think when the court sees what the numbers were generated and how that compares to her own standards for assessment, the court will see that the conclusion is not justified that she reached.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:15:47)</span>: All right. Well, I understand what both parties are saying and at a probable cause hearing the issue is whether we walk one mile or a hundred miles down the path. And so I want to keep it limited to one mile as opposed to a hundred miles because that would exceed the probable cause standard for a preliminary hearing for what is before me. And so Mr. Burt, as it relates to the objection, I'm going to sustain it in part. So we can move on to whatever else you would like to present.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:16:38)</span>: Thank you. We'll do that, Your Honor. <span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:18:10)</span>: Explain to the court how this works, what this chart is supposed to represent.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:18:14)</span>: Sure. So this is our verbal scale. In addition to the numerical likelihood ratio value that I provide, I also give a qualitative or a verbal equivalent to that numerical value that gives some kind of context to what that likelihood ratio means.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:18:31)</span>: Okay. And on the left, explain what the greater than one million less than one in a hundred means. What does that scale relate to?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:18:42)</span>: Sure. So that scale is the numerical value of the likelihood ratio. So if I have a likelihood ratio that's greater than or equal to one million, I designate that as very strong support for inclusion. The next example, 10,000 to less than one million, I would say that's very strong support for inclusion and so forth.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:19:01)</span>: So you need to reach a likelihood ratio of two in order to get even limited support for an inclusion, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:19:07)</span>: Correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:19:08)</span>: Okay.
