<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:19:51)</span>: It's not in the report?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:19:53)</span>: The chart at the bottom of the screen is not my comparison to Mr. Twiggs.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:20:04)</span>: Let's verify that if we could, take that down, the highlight and repost 38, page 45. I'm sorry. Page 39 that is in evidence. There we go. Second page. Is this the likelihood ratio from your Twiggs comparison?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:20:52)</span>: Yes, it is.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:20:52)</span>: Okay. Now what was the likelihood ratio that you calculated based on this chart?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:20:59)</span>: The likelihood ratio was one.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:21:03)</span>: So that doesn't rise to the level of even limited inclusion, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:21:08)</span>: Correct. It would fall under an uninformative.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:21:11)</span>: Uninformative. And yet you concluded that despite this STRmix reading with a likelihood ratio of one, that Mr. Twiggs was a contributor to both item seven and item eight, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:21:25)</span>: I did. So actually I didn't utilize this calculation to determine that Mr. Twiggs was a possible contributor or that I could use him as an assumed contributor. I visually looked at the DNA sample and the DNA, unlike that major contributor, he also had those peaks. So that was one proposition or one hypothesis that I determined that he was an assumed contributor. There are multiple different ways. We don't have a threshold. I don't have to see two, four, eight peaks to determine I think an individual is present given that the field submitted him as an elimination.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:22:03)</span>: When I compared him to that data, I did see his DNA present. So when I calculated the statistic for Mr. Robinson, I used Mr. Twiggs as an elimination.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:22:18)</span>: Let me see if I understand that. You relied on the STRmix to include Mr. Robinson as a possible contributor, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:22:34)</span>: Correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:22:35)</span>: But when you ran Mr. Twiggs' sample through STRmix, the result you got was that he was not a contributor, correct? Or that it was not even of limited value in including him?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:22:51)</span>: Based on the STRmix run, it was uninformative, but I did use my own knowledge and training to determine that I was going to utilize him as an assumed contributor.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:23:02)</span>: Okay. Your own knowledge and training, is there some standard that you use for deciding when not to rely on the STRmix results and when to sort of disregard it and rely on your training and experience?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:23:22)</span>: I can't say that I disregarded it. When I'm comparing someone, that's my first determination: do I think this person is potentially included or excluded? If I determine that I think they're included, I will run STRmix to give weight to that inclusion. Elimination samples are different. If I'm told that an individual is submitted as an elimination sample, I can assume they're there. Visually, I am able to look at the DNA evidence and determine, "Yes, I think there's enough evidence there to assume that individual," which is what I did here.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:23:56)</span>: Okay. At D16, Mr. Twiggs was a 912, right? According to your electropherograms?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:24:17)</span>: That is correct, yes. Mr. Twiggs was a 912 at D16.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:24:22)</span>: 912. And did you find 912 at that location in the 8-1 sample?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:24:32)</span>: I'm sorry, the sample eight or sample seven?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:24:35)</span>: Sample eight.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:24:49)</span>: The only piece of information I had for sample eight at item D16 was a 12.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:24:56)</span>: And you said Twiggs at that was a 912, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:24:59)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:25:00)</span>: But at that location, the evidence sample was not a 912, but a 12?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:25:06)</span>: That was the only piece of information that was recovered at that location.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:25:10)</span>: Let me ask you about your interpretation rules for excluding someone. Is this an accurate statement? This is from Butler's textbook. "In forensic DNA analysis, if any STR locus fails to match when comparing the genotypes between two or more samples, then the comparison of profiles between the question and the reference sample is usually declared a non-match regardless of how many other loci match."

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:25:48)</span>: I would agree with that if it's a single source sample. So single source, again, meaning from one individual. If I have a DNA profile from evidence and I compare it to a known individual, if there's a difference at even one location, I can say it's an exclusion. If I have a mixture, it's not that cut and dry. I have to take into account how many people are in that mixture, how many pieces of information I have, is there potential dropout? So just because someone may not be present in a mixture doesn't necessarily mean that I'm going to automatically exclude them.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:26:25)</span>: I see. At D19S, what was Mr. Twiggs' type?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:26:35)</span>: It was a 1416.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:26:37)</span>: Did you find a 16 allele in the 8A sample?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:26:42)</span>: No, I did not.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:26:48)</span>: At THO, what was Mr. Twiggs' type?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:26:54)</span>: He was a 79.3.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:26:56)</span>: Did you find a seven allele in the 8.1 sample?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:27:03)</span>: No, I did not. But again, this is a mixture and there is some dropout. So just because I didn't see a seven, for example, does not mean I would automatically exclude someone.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:27:13)</span>: It's a mixture, so there was some dropout. So did you assume that at that location that the allele that dropped out matched his allele?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:27:24)</span>: At that location, no. There's only one individual represented there. So when comparing an elimination, I would essentially ignore that location. I'm looking at the other locations where there was two individuals present.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:27:40)</span>: Okay. Now a couple more topics and then we're done. When you received the evidence, you got more than seven and eight, correct? You got a number of other samples?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:28:18)</span>: So initially we didn't have any information. So we processed that. Those are items that are good for DNA. Not having any information from the investigators, we processed them for DNA. <span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:28:41)</span>: Well, left behind by a bystander or according to your notes at page 20, "The backpack belongs to [Kirk](/People/charlie-kirk)'s detailees?"

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:28:59)</span>: Yes. Based on the individual that ... That was not my communication log, that was someone else within the laboratory. That was the information that they received.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:29:08)</span>: Before you received any samples, did you request to perform what's called destructive testing on the samples?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:29:19)</span>: Yes, I did. And destructive testing is essentially consuming the sample; cutting the cotton off of the Q-tip or the swab, subjecting it to testing, so there's nothing remaining. I have to request permission to consume prior to doing so, and I did in this case.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:29:37)</span>: And why did you do that?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:29:39)</span>: That is part of our protocols that we have to request permission to consume. So DNA, we can't see it. So when an item is swabbed, we don't know how much DNA is on that swab, if anything, or how that swab was used. Did someone just swab using one side of it? Did they roll it and use all sides? Because I don't know that, it's our practice to consume that swab. It's our best chance of getting any DNA evidence.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:30:05)</span>: And that's a determination that you made in this case before you even saw any of the evidence, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:30:12)</span>: Determination to consume? Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:30:16)</span>: Now remember I referred to that NRC report from 1996? Did they say a wrongfully accused person's best insurance against the possibility of being falsely incriminated is the opportunity to have the testing repeated, and such an opportunity should be provided whenever possible?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:30:37)</span>: Yes, that does say that.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:30:39)</span>: And does your laboratory have a policy, standard 7.4, which says, "Where possible, the laboratory shall retain or return a portion of the evidence sample or extract?"

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:30:55)</span>: That is correct. <span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:31:08)</span>: On some of the items, right?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:31:13)</span>: Well, we'll get to that in a minute. And that policy that I read is from your lab policy, right?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:32:38)</span>: Okay. So when you wrote in your report that those items were consumed, you mean the actual swab was consumed?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:32:52)</span>: Correct. So a swab is just like a Q-tip. So the cotton portion of that swab was cut off the wooden stick, placed into a tube, subjected to DNA testing. So we consider that consuming. But again, there is liquid at the end of that process that is returned to the field.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:33:09)</span>: And the normal procedure in terms of sample preservation would be something called sample splitting, right? You split the swab and you use half, you save half so that your testing results can be replicated and retested?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:33:24)</span>: So yes. However, again, I don't know how an item is swabbed. So even if I were to cut that swab in half, it could be that I'm testing the half that doesn't have any DNA and the other half may have some DNA, or the swab may have a little bit of DNA on one side and a lot on the other side. So for the best possible chance of getting a DNA profile, it is our practice to consume the entire swab.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:33:50)</span>: So for the first six items, those were swabs that were taken from the northeast corner of the Losee building, correct?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:35:49)</span>: And when you do that, you create a mixture, right?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:36:24)</span>: Okay. And in this case, when you did that, what you got was a complex mixture, right?

And then if you go down to the 71 towel sample, which is what the topic is of this report introduced by the state, you'll see that she found a 17 there as well measured only by the length of the DNA strand. And what she's going to testify to is at this location and five or six others, there are alleles, all of which measure here 17, but actually contain different sequences, and that her testing that she used does not measure for those differences.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:40:50)</span>: And so as it relates to probable cause ... <span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:41:11)</span>: So it comports because it goes to the ultimate opinion expressed in the report that the state is offering that the report offers reliable support that Mr. Robinson is a possible contributor. And we're asking the court to consider that the comparisons are only based on a limited number of length based comparisons and that there are sequence differences so that she cannot distinguish, and I believe she'll testify she cannot distinguish based on the testing that she did in at least five separate locations whether the evidence sample and Mr. Robinson's sample, or for that matter, Mr. Twiggs' sample, matches the sequence differences here.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:42:03)</span>: Okay.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:42:03)</span>: And this is my final area and I think I can do it very efficiently, and I think it is important for the court to consider, especially as it relates to the comparison to Mr. Robinson.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:42:15)</span>: Mr. McBride.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:42:16)</span>: And she is also going to testify that the analysis she does is based on looking at the totality and all of the different markers that match and are consistent, and that's how she comes to the opinion that she does. The point is there are explanations that are susceptible to different interpretations and arguments, but ultimately, we're going to have an expert hearing where all of the literature is going to be before the court and the court's going to determine if it meets a threshold of reliability for admission at trial. But at this stage, even granting that there may be some argument that may come from defense counsel's position, the court can't weigh it. This does not go to the probable cause determination. And I don't think it's relevant. And this exhibit in particular is not relevant without the testimony of the witness. And so the exhibit should not be admitted on its face either.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:43:09)</span>: But that's why I was trying to lay a foundation that she offered a proof to the court that she would testify that in fact there are these sequence differences at the six different locations relevant to the comparison to Mr. Robinson. And when the state says the court cannot weigh it, the court can weigh reliability. The court has ruled that. So we're simply asking the court to take into account the limited nature of these comparisons and the fact that she cannot, based on the testing that she performed, tell a court whether there are differences at these five locations, she can't match Mr. Robinson to the questioned samples because she didn't do sequence testing, and she'll also testify that the FBI is in the process of implementing this kind of testing.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:44:11)</span>: All right. Thank you to both sides. <span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:45:19)</span>: All right. So it is ... Mr. McBride?

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:45:22)</span>: Just on a second matter, which is I need to cross-examine this witness. She's got to leave town today. I would like 20 minutes to cross-examine the witness.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:45:31)</span>: All right. So Mr. Burt, I'll turn to you. This has been moved into evidence. Do you want it on the screens?

Just in the interest of efficiency, pull up the next one please. I want 35. Take that one down. And is this exhibit the same chart with what are called the isoalleles indicated?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:46:23)</span>: Yes, it is.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:46:24)</span>: And isoalleles is a term used to refer to these alleles that have different sequence, but the same length?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:46:31)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:46:32)</span>: So across the top there for allele 17 and D3, there is an allele 17A, allele 17B, allele 17C, all with the same length, but different sequences for each of those three.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:47:56)</span>: And as you look at these five areas, you can't distinguish the evidence samples from Mr. Robinson based on the sequence differences, can you?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:48:22)</span>: And your laboratory is involved in validating next generation sequencing, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:48:27)</span>: We utilize next generation sequencing for mitochondrial DNA, which is a different subset of DNA testing, but not for nuclear DNA testing, which is what we're talking about today.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:48:39)</span>: All right. But the people who market STRmix also market a sequence platform, do they not?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:49:27)</span>: Thank you.

## Cross-Examination — Amanda Bakker (Ryan McBride, State)

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:49:28)</span>: All right. So turning to the state. Mr. McBride.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:50:13)</span>: Good afternoon. I want to start by asking you what your training and experience is. First of all, do you have any education?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:50:21)</span>: Yes, I do. I have a bachelor's of science in biology from Salisbury University.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:50:27)</span>: And after your bachelor's in biology degree, what did you do for your career and then further education?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:50:37)</span>: Upon graduating from college, I began working at the FBI laboratory as a contractor. I was assigned to the Federal Convicted Offender Program, which was a program under the DNA unit. During that time, I did that for approximately a year and a half, it was my responsibility to open and inventory and barcode DNA collection kits sent in from federal agencies across the United States. I then became a biologist in the DNA casework unit. I did that for approximately six years. During that time, I did the actual testing of items of evidence. So swabbing, cutting those swabs, producing the DNA profile. And then in 2015, I accepted a position as a forensic examiner, which I am still today.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:51:25)</span>: So 11 years you've been doing more or less the same duties you have now. Is that right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:51:30)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:51:33)</span>: I want to ask you about your lab's accreditation. What is accreditation for a laboratory?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:51:39)</span>: Accreditation is a formal recognition that our laboratory is following a very strict set of standards. We're accredited by ANAB, the American National Standards Institute National Accreditation Board. And our accreditation shows that we have to follow standards, such as I have to have a certain education background, how we process evidence, how we store evidence. Every four years we're audited to those standards, meaning an outside body will come into our laboratory, look at my training records, look at our standard operating procedures to ensure that we're following those standards.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:52:22)</span>: And you mentioned your training. So what do you have to do as an examiner there, as a scientist there for your lab to maintain its accreditation?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:52:34)</span>: So for my training program, I had to complete a two-year training program. During that time I learned the science behind DNA testing, and I showed that I understood that science by completing oral board examinations and written examinations. I also then had to show that I can relay results, those scientific results to a jury by completing moot court exercises. And before becoming qualified, I then took something called a competency exam, or it's like a final examination, to show that I'm qualified to perform my duties. During that accreditation or that audit process, my training records were reviewed to make sure that I had the proper coursework, the proper college degree, as well as completing successfully that training program.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:53:24)</span>: And do you have to keep up with ongoing education to maintain your qualification?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:53:37)</span>: Yes. On a yearly basis, I have to complete at least eight hours of continuing education. And this can be done by attending conferences or lectures or reading scientific literature.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:53:50)</span>: Do you also have to undergo proficiency testing?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:53:53)</span>: Yes. And a proficiency test is a test that we receive from an outside vendor. I process that test like I would any other case and submit those answers to determine if I'm satisfactory or unsatisfactory. This tests not only me to see if I'm still proficient in my qualifications, but it also tests the laboratory process to ensure that we're getting accurate and reliable results.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:54:19)</span>: Now, as you discussed your work in this case, you talked about your work needing to be reviewed by another analyst. And is that true in every single case?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:54:30)</span>: Yes. In every one of our cases, they have to undergo a technical review in addition to an administrative review.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:54:38)</span>: And it sounds like during those technical reviews and administrative reviews, there's discussions between you and other analysts. Is that accurate?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:54:46)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:54:51)</span>: And I assume that training and learning goes both directions, that you're learning and you're also helping others understand your thought process which might train them as well. Is that accurate?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:55:00)</span>: That's true, yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:55:03)</span>: The tools you use, are the tools you use validated?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:55:06)</span>: Yes, they are.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:55:09)</span>: And what does that mean to have a tool that you use validated?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:55:12)</span>: So a validation is a thorough testing of a process or procedure. So before we subject any evidence to testing, we ensure that those process and procedures will work within our laboratory. Based on the validation, we create something called standard operating procedures, or SOPs. And these are kind of like cookbooks. It gives us step-by-step instructions on how to do our process and procedures. And then both myself as an examiner as well as the biologists, we're all trained to those standard operating procedures and tested to those standard operating procedures during proficiency tests.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:55:50)</span>: Now you've talked about a couple of the tools you use. One you call STRmix. And is that tool validated?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:56:02)</span>: Yes. It's validated both developmentally and internally. Developmentally, it's validated by the creators of the software. And then we, the FBI laboratory, also tested STRmix to ensure that we could use it within our laboratory and produce accurate and reliable results.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:56:17)</span>: Now, when you say it was developmentally validated, is that in a peer-reviewed study?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:56:23)</span>: Yes, it is.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:56:28)</span>: And how widely is STRmix used throughout the world?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:56:36)</span>: STRmix is used in approximately 120 laboratories, both domestically and internationally.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:56:59)</span>: I want to go back to some of the questions that you were asked on direct examination. You were asked about Bakker 8.4, which is the DOJ Uniform Language for Reporting and Testimony. It talks about language that you should use and language that you shouldn't use as an examiner and a scientist, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:57:32)</span>: That is correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:57:33)</span>: Now, I want to ask you a little bit about what you do when you have a conclusion like you did in Exhibit 31. The directive from the Department of Justice limits the adjectives you can use to describe the evidence and the conclusions that you reach. Is that accurate?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:58:00)</span>: Yes, it is.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:58:01)</span>: And the thinking behind that is to submit the statistical number and let the reader decide how they want to describe that number. Is that accurate?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:58:12)</span>: Yes, it is.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:58:14)</span>: So for example, if we can look at Exhibit 31... item seven, sub one is the swabbing from the towel from the [rifle](/Gun_Bullet/Mauser_30_06_Rifle). We have the number in a written form in the second paragraph beneath the heading. And then beneath that, you have a table, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:59:05)</span>: That is correct, yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:59:07)</span>: And the likelihood ratio gives the number. And that's stated in sentence form in the paragraph preceding the table, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:59:18)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:59:20)</span>: The only language you can use to describe what that number means from the DOJ directive is very strong support for inclusion, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:59:28)</span>: Correct. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:59:30)</span>: And the sentence there that says, "The DNA results from item 7.1 are 1.7 octillion times more likely if [Twiggs](/People/lance-twiggs) and [T. Robinson](/People/tyler-robinson) are contributors than if Twiggs and an unknown unrelated person are contributors." The reader can assign whatever adjective they want to that, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:59:52)</span>: Sure. Yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:59:53)</span>: The adjective you can use is very strong support.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:59:57)</span>: For inclusion, yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(07:59:58)</span>: For inclusion. All right. If we go to 8.1 ... same question there. The results from 8.1 are 30 quintillion times more likely if Twiggs and T. Robinson are contributors than if Twiggs and an unknown unrelated person are contributors. The adjective you're allowed to use is very strong support for inclusion again, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:00:25)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:00:25)</span>: Okay. We can take that exhibit down. You were asked about formal activity level and you described this as you cannot testify about what action left the DNA on the object. How can DNA get onto an object?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:01:11)</span>: DNA can get on an object via direct contact, so an individual touching an object. It can also end up on an item via body fluids such as blood or through transfer. So if I were to touch an object and then that object is given to someone else, my DNA could potentially be on that other individual through the transfer of DNA.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:01:34)</span>: And you cannot testify as to what precise action left the DNA on the object, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:01:45)</span>: Correct. I cannot.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:01:46)</span>: Okay. Same question about time. You can't testify about the exact time that DNA was left on an object, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:01:53)</span>: Correct.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:01:54)</span>: But there are some indicators that may give some information about how long and the conditions under which DNA was placed on an item, like degradation?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:02:10)</span>: Yes, degradation or the condition of the item. So again, if a firearm, for example, is in a river, I would expect with the water rushing over that item, I may or may not be able to detect DNA. Also, if there's an item of evidence that is exposed to the elements, something that's been left behind 10 years ago, I don't necessarily expect to recover DNA, but something that may have been left yesterday, I might be able to detect DNA.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:02:41)</span>: An item that has been left in the dirt or has been rubbed in the dirt, could that affect your ability to recover DNA?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:02:46)</span>: It could, yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:02:47)</span>: Could it also affect degradation?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:02:48)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:02:52)</span>: Can UV rays affect degradation?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:02:54)</span>: Yes, it can.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:03:00)</span>: Does degradation keep you from doing your work?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:03:03)</span>: No, it does not.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:03:05)</span>: Is degradation normal? Does all DNA degrade?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:03:12)</span>: It does, yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:03:13)</span>: I imagine if it's kept in a pristine environment, it would degrade less than if it's found out in the open. Is that accurate?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:03:25)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:03:25)</span>: Okay. You said with regard to the screwdriver, there is some degradation. Did that affect your ability to reach conclusions in this case?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:03:37)</span>: No, it did not.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:03:38)</span>: You said on item seven, the swab from the towel, there was slight degradation, not as much as Exhibit 8. Did that degradation affect your ability to reach a conclusion here?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:03:50)</span>: No, it did not.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:04:04)</span>: You were asked about a PCAST report. You testified that gave guidance on different areas of forensic science. Do you know what year that report was published?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:04:15)</span>: I don't recall the year. I think it was 2016, but I'm not entirely sure. I would say within the last 10 years.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:04:34)</span>: Let me ask you this. Has the science in your area — can I refer to it as probabilistic genotyping — developed over the last 10 years?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:04:54)</span>: Yes, it has.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:04:55)</span>: And have there been studies that have made suggestions which have been implemented in the field?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:05:01)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:05:08)</span>: You testified about items seven and eight that they both had mixtures. And you stated, "I believe that the minor contributor was less than 20% and that it was actually 5% and the ratio was 5% to 95%."

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:05:42)</span>: For one of the samples, yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:05:45)</span>: Does that mean the 5% was from Twiggs and the 95% is assumed from the defendant, Robinson?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:05:58)</span>: When I calculated the statistic, Twiggs' DNA profile aligned with the 5% contributor and Mr. Robinson's DNA profile aligned better with the 95% contributor.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:06:10)</span>: Okay. Same question with regard to eight. I think you said the ratio was 11% from the minor and 89% from the major. Is that Twiggs being the 11% and Robinson being the 89%?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:06:24)</span>: Yes. Twiggs better aligned with that 11% contributor and Mr. Robinson better aligned with that 89% contributor.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:07:22)</span>: Thank you. It's 5:00. I'm going to wrap up. In reaching your conclusions, did you follow the protocols of your accredited lab?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:08:04)</span>: Yes, I did.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:08:05)</span>: And based on your training and experience in following those protocols, did you in fact come to the conclusions described in Exhibit 31?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:08:16)</span>: Yes, I did.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:08:17)</span>: Thank you.

## Redirect — Amanda Bakker (Michael Burt)

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:08:20)</span>: All right. Mr. Burt? And how much time are you anticipating?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:08:34)</span>: Five minutes. You referred on cross-examination to your training and experience, right? Do you agree with this statement in the PCAST report? "Experience or judgment cannot be used to establish the scientific validity and reliability of a metrological method such as a forensic feature comparison method. Moreover, a forensic examiner's experience from extensive casework is not informative, because the right answers are not typically known in casework, and thus examiners cannot accurately know how often they erroneously declare matches and cannot readily hone their accuracy by learning from their mistakes in the course of casework." Do you agree with that statement?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:09:23)</span>: I don't. They're right in the fact that we never know the true answer. However, my experience means that I've seen a lot of different DNA. I've seen a lot of validation data, so that does help me come to a conclusion.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:09:41)</span>: Do you agree with this statement by that same report? "Good professional practices such as the existence of professional societies, certification programs, accreditation programs, peer-reviewed articles, standardized protocols, proficiency testing and the code of ethics cannot substitute for actual evidence of scientific validity and reliability."

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:10:09)</span>: I don't think I agree with that either. The science has been heavily validated and tested to show that's reliable. Following protocols that are based on validation, again, shows that my results are accurate and reliable as well as being proficiency tested.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:10:29)</span>: By the way, you said that report was not authored by experts, but the head of that group was Eric Lander, who is one of the foremost experts in the area of forensic biology. Is he not?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:10:44)</span>: That name does not ring a bell. I don't know who that individual is.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:10:47)</span>: You don't know who Eric Lander is?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:10:49)</span>: No, I do not.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:10:49)</span>: Okay. When did you go to school? What years?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:10:53)</span>: 2003 to 2007.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:10:56)</span>: You said that STRmix was widely used and it's been validated. The PCAST report said the problem is it's been validated by the people selling the product, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:11:07)</span>: Developmental validation, yes. Internal validation, no. A laboratory that's going to utilize STRmix has to validate within their own laboratory.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:11:16)</span>: And the PCAST report said that we need validation data, not by the people selling the product, but by independent scientists in order to make this reliable, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:11:27)</span>: They do, and that's exactly what happened. There was 31 laboratories that came together in response to the PCAST report and gave their validation data on how they internally validated STRmix.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:11:40)</span>: And then after that information came forward, the PCAST committee reviewed it and they said, "PCAST was unpersuaded by those studies. While likelihood ratios are a mathematically sound concept, their application requires making a set of assumptions about DNA profiles that require empirical testing. Errors in the assumptions can lead to errors in the results. To establish validity with a range of parameters is thus important to undertake empirical testing with a variety of samples in the relevant range." And they specifically address the studies you're referring to, did they not?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:12:21)</span>: I believe they did, yes.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:12:23)</span>: Yeah. And then lastly, you said that STRmix is widely used, but as I understand your testimony, you didn't use it and rely upon it for your conclusion about [Twiggs](/People/lance-twiggs), right? The software told you there was no support there for including him and you ignored it and decided otherwise, correct?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:12:48)</span>: I did not ignore it. Again, given the type of sample, it was an elimination sample. I compared that individual to the DNA profile that I developed and determined that I could conclude that I could utilize him as an elimination sample.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:13:06)</span>: You said your laboratory was accredited, correct? But you're aware that in 2004, the Inspector General did a comprehensive audit of your laboratory and published a report called The FBI DNA Laboratory: A Review of Protocol and Practice Vulnerabilities, which found a number of errors in the way that your lab does testing, correct?

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:13:31)</span>: Objection. The 2004 study is hardly relevant to this proceeding 22 years later.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:13:37)</span>: It's relevant because he's relying on accreditation to somehow say that these results are valid. This lab was accredited when this study was done.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:13:47)</span>: I'll allow this single question as she may respond, so I'm overruling the objection and then we'll continue to move forward.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:13:53)</span>: You're aware of that study, right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:13:55)</span>: No, I'm not.

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:13:56)</span>: Okay. Thank you. That's all I have.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:13:58)</span>: All right. Anything further for this witness?

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:02)</span>: Yes. One follow-up. You testified that you never know the true answer with field work, correct? And that's compared to ground truth studies where you do know the true answer and you can compare the conclusions that analysts draw with the true answer, the ground truth answer that is the basis of the study. Is that right?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:24)</span>: Yes. And that is what our validation is.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:27)</span>: Your validation actually is a ground truth test in essence?

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:32)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:32)</span>: Okay. Thank you.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:34)</span>: Anything further for this witness?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:36)</span>: No. Thank you, Ms. Bakker.

<span style={{color:'#17458f', fontWeight:600}}>Ms. Bakker</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:38)</span>: Thank you.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:39)</span>: May this witness be excused?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:40)</span>: Yes.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:41)</span>: All right. Thank you, Ms. Bakker. You may step down.

*(The witness was excused.)*

## Adjournment

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:44)</span>: All right. Well, does either party need the benefit of the record before we go into recess?

<span style={{color:'#c05a12', fontWeight:600}}>Michael Burt</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:48)</span>: No.

<span style={{color:'#6a1b9a', fontWeight:600}}>Mr. McBride</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:51)</span>: No objection.

<span style={{color:'#1a1a1a', fontWeight:700}}>Judge</span> <span style={{color:'#777', fontSize:'0.8em'}}>(08:14:51)</span>: All right. Having both parties say no, we'll go ahead and be adjourned until tomorrow at 1:00 PM. We do not have morning court for this proceeding and we'll start up at 1:00 PM in the afternoon. The court is in recess.

*(The proceedings adjourned until 1:00 PM the following day. Sergeant Jennifer Faumuina's testimony remained paused, to resume with cross-examination.)*
